width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Universe</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Universe_(disambiguation)" title="Universe (disambiguation)">Universe (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">all of space and time and their contents</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Universe</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg"><img alt="NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="2300" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg/300px-NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg/450px-NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg/600px-NASA-HS201427a-HubbleUltraDeepField2014-20140603.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a><div>The <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">Hubble Ultra-Deep Field</a> image shows some of the most remote <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> visible with present technology, each consisting of billions of stars. (Apparent image area about 1/79 that of a full moon)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacetelescope.org_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-spacetelescope.org-1">[1]</a></sup></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">Age</a> (within <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>)</th><td>13.799 &#177; 0.021 billion years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Diameter</th><td>Unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Diameter of the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7026880000000000000&#9824;</span>8.8<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>26</sup>&#160;m</span> <span class="nowrap">(28.5 G<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a> or 93 G<a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Mass (ordinary matter)</th><td>At least 10<sup>53</sup> kg<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Davies_2006_43_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Davies_2006_43-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Average density (including the contribution from <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>)</th><td>9.9 x 10<sup>&#8722;30</sup> g/cm<sup>3</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap_universe_made_of_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap_universe_made_of-6">[6]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Average temperature</th><td>2.72548 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fixsen_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fixsen-7">[7]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Main contents</th><td><a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">Ordinary (baryonic)</a> <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> (4.9%)<br/><a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a> (26.8%)<br/><a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a> (68.3%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck2013parameters_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck2013parameters-8">[8]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Shape</th><td><a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">Flat</a> with only a 0.4% margin of error<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Universe</b> is all of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacetime_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-spacetime-10">[a]</a></sup> and their contents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeilik1998_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeilik1998-11">[10]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxies" title="Galaxies">galaxies</a>, and all other forms of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. While the spatial size of the entire Universe is unknown,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-3">[3]</a></sup> it is possible to measure the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>.\n</p><p>The earliest scientific models of the Universe were developed by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_philosophers" title="Indian philosophers">Indian philosophers</a> and were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentric" title="Geocentric">geocentric</a>, placing <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> at the center of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-13">[12]</a></sup> Over the centuries, more precise astronomical observations led <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> to develop the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_model" title="Heliocentric model">heliocentric model</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> at the center of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. In developing the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton\'s law of universal gravitation">law of universal gravitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> built upon Copernicus\' work as well as observations by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion">laws of planetary motion</a>.\n</p><p>Further observational improvements led to the realization that the Sun is one of hundreds of billions of stars in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, which is one of at least hundreds of billions of galaxies in the Universe. Many of the stars in our galaxy <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">have planets</a>. <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#End_of_Greatness" title="Observable universe">At the largest scale</a> galaxies are distributed uniformly and the same in all directions, meaning that the Universe has neither an edge nor a center. At smaller scales, galaxies are distributed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_cluster" title="Galactic cluster">clusters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">superclusters</a> which form immense <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">filaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a> in space, creating a vast foam-like structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Discoveries in the early 20th century have suggested that the Universe had a beginning and that space has been expanding since then,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawking_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawking-15">[14]</a></sup> and is currently still expanding at an increasing rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobel_2011_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_2011-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory is the prevailing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological" title="Cosmological">cosmological</a> description of the development of the Universe. Under this theory, space and time emerged together <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001137990000000000&#9824;</span>13.799<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.021&#160;billion years</span> ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-2">[2]</a></sup> with a fixed amount of energy and matter that has become less dense as the Universe has expanded. After an <a href="/wiki/Inflationary_epoch" title="Inflationary epoch">initial accelerated expansion</a> at around 10<sup>&#8722;32</sup> seconds, and the separation of the four known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a>, the Universe gradually cooled and continued to expand, allowing the first <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a> and simple <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> to form. <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a> gradually gathered forming a <a href="/wiki/Foam" title="Foam">foam</a>-like structure of <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">filaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a> under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Giant clouds of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> were gradually drawn to the places where dark matter was most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dense" title="Dense">dense</a>, forming the first galaxies, stars, and everything else seen today. It is possible to see objects that are now further away than 13.799 billion <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> because <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">space itself has expanded</a>, and it is still expanding today. This means that objects which are now up to 46.5 billion light-years away can <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">still be seen</a> in their distant past, because in the past when their light was emitted, they were much closer to the Earth.\n</p><p>From studying the movement of galaxies, it has been discovered that the universe contains much more <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> than is accounted for by visible objects; stars, galaxies, nebulas and interstellar gas. This unseen matter is known as dark matter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> (<i>dark</i> means that there is a wide range of strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indirect_evidence" title="Indirect evidence">indirect evidence</a> that it exists, but we have not yet detected it directly). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9BCDM" title="&#923;CDM">&#923;CDM</a> model is the most widely accepted model of our universe. It suggests that about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999692000000000000&#9824;</span>69.2%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>1.2%</span> [2015] of the mass and energy in the universe is a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> (or, in extensions to &#923;CDM, other forms of <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">scalar field</a>) which is responsible for the current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expansion_of_space" title="Expansion of space">expansion of space</a>, and about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999258000000000000&#9824;</span>25.8%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>1.1%</span> [2015] is dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_2015_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_2015-18">[17]</a></sup> Ordinary ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic</a>") matter is therefore only 4.9% [2015] of the physical universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_2015_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-planck_2015-18">[17]</a></sup> Stars, planets, and visible gas clouds only form about 6% of ordinary matter, or about 0.3% of the entire universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are many competing hypotheses about the <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe" title="Ultimate fate of the universe">ultimate fate of the universe</a> and about what, if anything, preceded the Big Bang, while other physicists and philosophers refuse to speculate, doubting that information about prior states will ever be accessible. Some physicists have suggested various <a href="/wiki/Multiverse" title="Multiverse">multiverse</a> hypotheses, in which the Universe might be one among many universes that likewise exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EllisKS032_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-EllisKS032-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>\n<p>The physical Universe is defined as all of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacetime_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-spacetime-10">[a]</a></sup> (collectively referred to as <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>) and their contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeilik1998_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeilik1998-11">[10]</a></sup> Such contents comprise all of energy in its various forms, including <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>, and therefore planets, <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, stars, galaxies, and the contents of <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">intergalactic space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> The Universe also includes the <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws</a> that influence energy and matter, such as <a href="/wiki/Conservation_law" title="Conservation law">conservation laws</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schreuder2014_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schreuder2014-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Universe is often defined as "the totality of existence", or <a href="/wiki/Everything" title="Everything">everything</a> that exists, everything that has existed, and everything that will exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schreuder2014_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schreuder2014-25">[24]</a></sup> In fact, some philosophers and scientists support the inclusion of ideas and abstract concepts &#8211; such as mathematics and logic &#8211; in the definition of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> The word <i>universe</i> may also refer to concepts such as <i>the cosmos</i>, <i>the world</i>, and <i>nature</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bolonkin2011_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bolonkin2011-31">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The word <i>universe</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> word <i>univers</i>, which in turn derives from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>universum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> The Latin word was used by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and later Latin authors in many of the same senses as the modern <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> word is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_short_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis_short-33">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synonyms">Synonyms</span></h3>\n<p>A term for "universe" among the ancient Greek philosophers from <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> onwards was <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#964;&#8056; &#960;&#8118;&#957;</span>, <i>t&#242; p&#226;n</i> ("the all"), defined as all matter and all space, and <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#964;&#8056; &#8005;&#955;&#959;&#957;</span>, <i>t&#242; h&#243;lon</i> ("all things"), which did not necessarily include the void.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Another synonym was <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#8001; &#954;&#972;&#963;&#956;&#959;&#962;</span>, <i>ho k&#243;smos</i> (meaning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_(philosophy)" title="World (philosophy)">world</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Synonyms are also found in Latin authors (<i>totum</i>, <i>mundus</i>, <i>natura</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> and survive in modern languages, e.g., the German words <i>Das All</i>, <i>Weltall</i>, and <i>Natur</i> for <i>Universe</i>. The same synonyms are found in English, such as everything (as in the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">theory of everything</a>), the cosmos (as in <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>), the world (as in the <a href="/wiki/Many-worlds_interpretation" title="Many-worlds interpretation">many-worlds interpretation</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> (as in <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural laws</a> or <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology_and_the_Big_Bang">Chronology and the Big Bang</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">Chronology of the universe</a></div>\n<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10;
