position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div></div></div><div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div><div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><div style="float: left;"><small><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">billion years</a></small></div><br/><span style="float:right;margin-right:0.5em"><img alt="The image above contains clickable links" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="133" decoding="async" height="27" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/18px-Interactive_icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/27px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/36px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 2x" title="The image above contains clickable links" width="18"/></span><div style="float: left;"><small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">Life timeline</a></i></small></div></div></div>\n<p>The prevailing model for the evolution of the Universe is the Big Bang theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The Big Bang model states that the earliest state of the Universe was an extremely hot and dense one, and that the Universe subsequently expanded and cooled. The model is based on <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> and on simplifying assumptions such as <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)#Translation_invariance" title="Homogeneity (physics)">homogeneity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropy</a> of space. A version of the model with a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> (Lambda) and <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>, is the simplest model that provides a reasonably good account of various observations about the Universe. The Big Bang model accounts for observations such as the correlation of distance and <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of galaxies, the ratio of the number of hydrogen to helium atoms, and the microwave radiation background.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/280px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/420px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/560px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>In this diagram, time passes from left to right, so at any given time, the Universe is represented by a disk-shaped "slice" of the diagram.</center></div></div></div>\n<p>The initial hot, dense state is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_epoch" title="Planck epoch">Planck epoch</a>, a brief period extending from time zero to one <a href="/wiki/Planck_time" title="Planck time">Planck time</a> unit of approximately 10<sup>&#8722;43</sup> seconds. During the Planck epoch, all types of matter and all types of energy were concentrated into a dense state, and <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> - currently the weakest by far of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_interactions" title="Fundamental interactions">four known forces</a> - is believed to have been as strong as the other fundamental forces, and all the forces may have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unification" title="Grand unification">unified</a>. Since the Planck epoch, space has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expanding</a> to its present scale, with a very short but intense period of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a> believed to have occurred within the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_Notation" title="Scientific Notation">10<sup>&#8722;32</sup></a> seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sivaram_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sivaram-41">[40]</a></sup> This was a kind of expansion different from those we can see around us today. Objects in space did not physically move; instead the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">metric</a> that defines space itself changed. Although objects in <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> cannot move faster than the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, this limitation does not apply to the metric governing spacetime itself. This initial period of inflation is believed to explain why space appears to be very flat, and much larger than light could travel since the start of the universe.\n</p><p>Within the first fraction of a second of the universe\'s existence, the four fundamental forces had separated. As the universe continued to cool down from its inconceivably hot state, various types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subatomic_particles" title="Subatomic particles">subatomic particles</a> were able to form in short periods of time known as the <a href="/wiki/Quark_epoch" title="Quark epoch">quark epoch</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hadron_epoch" title="Hadron epoch">hadron epoch</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lepton_epoch" title="Lepton epoch">lepton epoch</a>. Together, these epochs encompassed less than 10 seconds of time following the Big Bang. These <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a> associated stably into ever larger combinations, including stable <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, which then formed more complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a> through <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. This process, known as <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>, only lasted for about 17 minutes and ended about 20 minutes after the Big Bang, so only the fastest and simplest reactions occurred. About 25% of the <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and all the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> in the universe, by mass, were converted to <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, with small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">form</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>) and traces of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>. Any other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> was only formed in very tiny quantities. The other 75% of the protons remained unaffected, as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> nuclei.\n</p><p>After nucleosynthesis ended, the universe entered a period known as the <a href="/wiki/Photon_epoch" title="Photon epoch">photon epoch</a>. During this period, the Universe was still far too hot for matter to form neutral <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, so it contained a hot, dense, foggy <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> of negatively charged <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, neutral <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> and positive nuclei. After about 377,000 years, the universe had cooled enough that electrons and nuclei could form the first stable <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>. This is known as <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination</a> for historical reasons; in fact electrons and nuclei were combining for the first time. Unlike plasma, neutral atoms are <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">transparent</a> to many <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of light, so for the first time the universe also became transparent. The photons released ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photon_decoupling" title="Photon decoupling">decoupled</a>") when these atoms formed can still be seen today; they form the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> (CMB).\n</p><p>As the Universe expands, the <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> decreases more quickly than does that of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> because the energy of a photon decreases with its wavelength. At around 47,000 years, the <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of matter became larger than that of photons and <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>, and began to dominate the large scale behavior of the universe. This marked the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation-dominated_era" title="Radiation-dominated era">radiation-dominated era</a> and the start of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matter-dominated_era" title="Matter-dominated era">matter-dominated era</a>.\n</p><p>In the earliest stages of the universe, tiny fluctuations within the universe\'s density led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filament_(cosmology)" title="Filament (cosmology)">concentrations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> gradually forming. Ordinary matter, attracted to these by <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, formed large gas clouds and eventually, stars and galaxies, where the dark matter was most dense, and <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a> where it was least dense. After around 100 - 300 million years,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the first <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> formed, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_III" title="Population III">Population III</a> stars. These were probably very massive, luminous, <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">non metallic</a> and short-lived. They were responsible for the gradual <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">reionization</a> of the Universe between about 200-500 million years and 1 billion years, and also for seeding the universe with elements heavier than helium, through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> The Universe also contains a mysterious energy - possibly a <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">scalar field</a> - called <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>, the density of which does not change over time. After about 9.8 billion years, the Universe had expanded sufficiently so that the density of matter was less than the density of dark energy, marking the beginning of the present <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark-energy-dominated_era" title="Dark-energy-dominated era">dark-energy-dominated era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> In this era, the expansion of the Universe is <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the universe">accelerating</a> due to dark energy.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">Observable universe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" title="Age of the Universe">Age of the Universe</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">Metric expansion of space</a></div>\n<p>Of the four <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> is the dominant at astronomical length scales. Gravity\'s effects are cumulative; by contrast, the effects of positive and negative charges tend to cancel one another, making electromagnetism relatively insignificant on astronomical length scales. The remaining two interactions, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" title="Weak nuclear force">weak</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_force" title="Strong nuclear force">strong nuclear forces</a>, decline very rapidly with distance; their effects are confined mainly to sub-atomic length scales.\n</p><p>The Universe appears to have much more <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> than <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>, an asymmetry possibly related to the <a href="/wiki/CP_violation" title="CP violation">CP violation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> This imbalance between matter and antimatter is partially responsible for the existence of all matter existing today, since matter and antimatter, if equally produced at the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, would have completely annihilated each other and left only <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> as a result of their interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TT-20171025_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-TT-20171025-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20171020_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20171020-46">[45]</a></sup> The Universe also appears to have neither net <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>, which follows accepted physical laws if the Universe is finite. These laws are the <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law" title="Gauss\'s law">Gauss\'s law</a> and the non-divergence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress-energy-momentum_pseudotensor" title="Stress-energy-momentum pseudotensor">stress-energy-momentum pseudotensor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin:0.5em auto; width:800px;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Constituent spatial scales of the observable universe\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="font-size:88%"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Location_of_Earth_(3x3-English_Annot-smaller).png"><img alt="Location of Earth (3x3-English Annot-smaller).png" data-file-height="3294" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="586" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Location_of_Earth_%283x3-English_Annot-smaller%29.png/800px-Location_of_Earth_%283x3-English_Annot-smaller%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Location_of_Earth_%283x3-English_Annot-smaller%29.png/1200px-Location_of_Earth_%283x3-English_Annot-smaller%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Location_of_Earth_%283x3-English_Annot-smaller%29.png/1600px-Location_of_Earth_%283x3-English_Annot-smaller%29.png 2x" width="800"/></a>\n<center>This diagram shows Earth\'s location in the Universe on increasingly larger scales.  The images, labeled along their left edge, increase in size from right to left, then from top to bottom. </center>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_and_regions">Size and regions</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">Observable universe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Observational_cosmology" title="Observational cosmology">Observational cosmology</a></div>\n<p>The size of the Universe is somewhat difficult to define. According to the general theory of relativity, far regions of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> may never interact with ours even in the lifetime of the Universe due to the finite <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> and the ongoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expansion_of_space" title="Expansion of space">expansion of space</a>. For example, radio messages sent from Earth may never reach some regions of space, even if the Universe were to exist forever: space may expand faster than light can traverse it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaku2008_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaku2008-48">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Distant regions of space are assumed to exist and to be part of reality as much as we are, even though we can never interact with them. The spatial region that we can affect and be affected by is the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>. The observable universe depends on the location of the observer. By traveling, an observer can come into contact with a greater region of spacetime than an observer who remains still. Nevertheless, even the most rapid traveler will not be able to interact with all of space. Typically, the observable universe is taken to mean the portion of the Universe that is observable from our vantage point in the Milky Way.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">proper distance</a>&#8212;the distance as would be measured at a specific time, including the present&#8212;between <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and the edge of the observable universe is 46 billion light-years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extra_Dimensions_in_Space_and_Time_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extra_Dimensions_in_Space_and_Time-49">[48]</a></sup> (14 billion parsecs),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> making the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Size" title="Observable universe">diameter of the observable universe</a> about 93 billion light-years (28 billion parsecs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extra_Dimensions_in_Space_and_Time_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Extra_Dimensions_in_Space_and_Time-49">[48]</a></sup> The distance the light from the edge of the observable universe has travelled is very close to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" title="Age of the Universe">age of the Universe</a> times the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, 13.8&#160;billion light-years (4.2<span style="margin-left:0.2em">&#215;<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;pc), but this does not represent the distance at any given time because the edge of the observable universe and the Earth have since moved further apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> For comparison, the diameter of a typical <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> is 30,000 light-years (9,198 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parsecs" title="Parsecs">parsecs</a>), and the typical distance between two neighboring galaxies is 3 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-years" title="Light-years">light-years</a> (919.8 kiloparsecs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r196_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-r196-52">[51]</a></sup> As an example, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> is roughly 100,000&#8211;180,000 light-years in diameter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> and the nearest sister galaxy to the Milky Way, the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, is located roughly 2.5 million light-years away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because we cannot observe space beyond the edge of the observable universe, it is unknown whether the size of the Universe in its totality is finite or infinite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brian_Greene_2011_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Greene_2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Estimates for the total size of the universe, if finite, reach as high as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 10^{10^{10^{122}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>122</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 10^{10^{10^{122}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="10^{10^{10^{122}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f0da4c6feb5287d705dc0f51c21317c5ddbce34b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.835ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span> megaparsecs, implied by one resolution of the No-Boundary Proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bignumber_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-bignumber-59">[b]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_and_expansion">Age and expansion</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">Age of the universe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">Metric expansion of space</a></div>\n<p>Astronomers calculate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" title="Age of the Universe">age of the Universe</a> by assuming that the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a> accurately describes the evolution of the Universe from a very uniform, hot, dense primordial state to its present state and measuring the cosmological parameters which constitute the model.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This model is well understood theoretically and supported by recent high-precision <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_observation" title="Astronomical observation">astronomical observations</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_(satellite)" title="Planck (satellite)">Planck</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Commonly, the set of observations fitted includes the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> anisotropy, the brightness/redshift relation for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernovae" title="Type Ia supernovae">Type Ia supernovae</a>, and large-scale galaxy clustering including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryon_acoustic_oscillation" title="Baryon acoustic oscillation">baryon acoustic oscillation</a> feature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other observations, such as the Hubble constant, the abundance of galaxy clusters, <a href="/wiki/Weak_gravitational_lensing" title="Weak gravitational lensing">weak gravitational lensing</a> and globular cluster ages, are generally consistent with these, providing a check of the model, but are less accurately measured at present.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Assuming that the Lambda-CDM model is correct, the measurements of the parameters using a variety of techniques by numerous experiments yield a best value of the age of the Universe as of 2015 of 13.799 <a href="/wiki/Measurement_uncertainty" title="Measurement uncertainty">&#177;</a> 0.021 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5100" data-file-width="3910" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg/220px-Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg/330px-Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg/440px-Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galactic_Cntr_full_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronomers discovered stars in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy that are almost 13.6 billion years old.</div></div></div>\n<p>Over time, the Universe and its contents have evolved; for example, the relative population of <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a> and galaxies has changed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> itself has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expanded</a>. Due to this expansion, scientists on Earth can observe the light from a galaxy 30 billion light-years away even though that light has traveled for only 13 billion years; the very space between them has expanded. This expansion is consistent with the observation that the light from distant galaxies has been <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifted</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> emitted have been stretched to longer <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> and lower <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> during their journey. Analyses of <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia supernovae</a> indicate that the spatial expansion is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_Universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the Universe">accelerating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riess_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-riess-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter-62">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The more matter there is in the Universe, the stronger the mutual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational" title="Gravitational">gravitational</a> pull of the matter. If the Universe were <i>too</i> dense then it would re-collapse into a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">gravitational singularity</a>. However, if the Universe contained too <i>little</i> matter then the self-gravity would be too weak for astronomical structures, like galaxies or planets, to form. Since the Big Bang, the universe has expanded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotonic" title="Monotonic">monotonically</a>. <a href="/wiki/Anthropic_principle#Anthropic_coincidences" title="Anthropic principle">Perhaps unsurprisingly</a>, our universe has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_Mass_Density_of_the_Universe" title="Critical Mass Density of the Universe">just the right mass-energy density</a>, equivalent to about 5 protons per cubic meter, which has allowed it to expand for the last 13.8 billion years, giving time to form the universe as observed today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are dynamical forces acting on the particles in the Universe which affect the expansion rate. Before 1998, it was expected that the expansion rate would be decreasing as time went on due to the influence of gravitational interactions in the Universe; and thus there is an additional observable quantity in the Universe called the <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">deceleration parameter</a>, which most cosmologists expected to be positive and related to the matter density of the Universe. In 1998, the deceleration parameter was measured by two different groups to be negative, approximately -0.55, which technically implies that the second derivative of the cosmic <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Scale_factor_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Scale factor cosmology (page does not exist)">scale factor</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ddot {a}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>&#168;<!-- &#168; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ddot {a}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ddot {a}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6b4d8db669dea9bee5d348bbecf6434914a406ac" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.23ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> has been positive in the last 5-6 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobel_2011_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_2011-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> This acceleration does not, however, imply that the Hubble parameter is currently increasing; see <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">deceleration parameter</a> for details.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacetime">Spacetime</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">Spacetime</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_line" title="World line">World line</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_transformation" title="Lorentz transformation">Lorentz transformation</a></div>\n<p>Spacetimes are the arenas in which all physical events take place. The basic elements of spacetimes are <a href="/wiki/Event_(relativity)" title="Event (relativity)">events</a>. In any given spacetime, an event is defined as a unique position at a unique time. A spacetime is the union of all events (in the same way that a line is the union of all of its points), formally organized into a <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">manifold</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Universe appears to be a smooth spacetime continuum consisting of three <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">spatial</a> <a href="/wiki/Dimension" title="Dimension">dimensions</a> and one temporal (<a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>) dimension (an event in the spacetime of the physical Universe can therefore be identified by a set of four coordinates: <span class="nowrap">(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i>, <i>t</i>)</span> ). On the average, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-space" title="3-space">space</a> is observed to be very nearly <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">flat</a> (with a <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a> close to zero), meaning that <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> is empirically true with high accuracy throughout most of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shape_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shape-66">[64]</a></sup> Spacetime also appears to have a <a href="/wiki/Simply_connected_space" title="Simply connected space">simply connected</a> <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>, in analogy with a sphere, at least on the length-scale of the observable Universe. However, present observations cannot exclude the possibilities that the Universe has more dimensions (which is postulated by theories such as the <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">String theory</a>) and that its spacetime may have a multiply connected global topology, in analogy with the cylindrical or <a href="/wiki/Toroid" title="Toroid">toroidal</a> topologies of two-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">spaces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nat03_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nat03-67">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-_spacetime_topology_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-_spacetime_topology-68">[66]</a></sup>\nThe spacetime of the Universe is usually interpreted from a <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean</a> perspective, with space as consisting of <a href="/wiki/Three-dimensional_space" title="Three-dimensional space">three dimensions</a>, and time as consisting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_dimension" title="One dimension">one dimension</a>, the "<a href="/wiki/Four-dimensional_space" title="Four-dimensional space">fourth dimension</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> By combining space and time into a single <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">manifold</a> called <a href="/wiki/Minkowski_space" title="Minkowski space">Minkowski space</a>, physicists have simplified a large number of <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">physical theories</a>, as well as described in a more uniform way the workings of the Universe at both the <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">supergalactic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">subatomic</a> levels.\n</p><p>Spacetime <a href="/wiki/Event_(relativity)" title="Event (relativity)">events</a> are not absolutely defined spatially and temporally but rather are known to be relative to the motion of an <a href="/wiki/Observer_(special_relativity)" title="Observer (special relativity)">observer</a>. Minkowski space approximates the Universe without <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Riemannian_manifold" title="Pseudo-Riemannian manifold">pseudo-Riemannian manifolds</a> of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> describe spacetime with matter and gravity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shape">Shape</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">Shape of the universe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:End_of_universe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="557" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/End_of_universe.jpg/220px-End_of_universe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/End_of_universe.jpg/330px-End_of_universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/End_of_universe.jpg/440px-End_of_universe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:End_of_universe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The three possible options for the shape of the Universe</div></div></div>\n<p>General relativity describes how spacetime is curved and bent by mass and energy (gravity). The <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> of the Universe includes both <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Local_geometry_(spatial_curvature)" title="Shape of the universe">local geometry</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Global_geometry" title="Shape of the universe">global geometry</a>.\nCosmologists often work with a given <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space-like" title="Space-like">space-like</a> slice of spacetime called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">comoving coordinates</a>. The section of spacetime which can be observed is the backward <a href="/wiki/Light_cone" title="Light cone">light cone</a>, which delimits the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_horizon" title="Cosmological horizon">cosmological horizon</a>.\nThe cosmological horizon (also called the particle horizon or the light horizon) is the maximum distance from which <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">particles</a> can have traveled to the <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observer</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Universe" title="Age of the Universe">age of the Universe</a>. This horizon represents the boundary between the observable and the unobservable regions of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> The existence, properties, and significance of a cosmological horizon depend on the particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological_model" title="Cosmological model">cosmological model</a>.\n</p><p>An important parameter determining the future evolution of the Universe theory is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Density_parameter" title="Density parameter">density parameter</a>, Omega (&#937;), defined as the average matter density of the universe divided by a critical value of that density. This selects one of three possible <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">geometries</a> depending on whether &#937; is equal to, less than, or greater than 1. These are called, respectively, the flat, open and closed universes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FateOfTheUniverse_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FateOfTheUniverse-72">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Observations, including the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">Cosmic Background Explorer</a> (COBE), <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP), and <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">Planck</a> maps of the CMB, suggest that the Universe is infinite in extent with a finite age, as described by the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker</a> (FLRW) models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_popular_uni_curv_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_popular_uni_curv-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nat03_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nat03-67">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RBSG08_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-RBSG08-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aurich0403597_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aurich0403597-75">[73]</a></sup> These FLRW models thus support inflationary models and the standard model of cosmology, describing a <a href="/wiki/Minkowski_space" title="Minkowski space">flat</a>, homogeneous universe presently dominated by <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_cosmological_parameters_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_cosmological_parameters-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_of_life">Support of life</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fine-tuned_Universe" title="Fine-tuned Universe">Fine-tuned Universe</a></div>\n<p>The Universe may be <i>fine-tuned</i>; the Fine-tuned Universe hypothesis is the proposition that the conditions that allow the existence of observable <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> in the Universe can only occur when certain universal <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">fundamental physical constants</a> lie within a very narrow range of values, so that if any of several fundamental constants were only slightly different, the Universe would have been unlikely to be conducive to the establishment and development of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>, astronomical structures, elemental diversity, or life as it is understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toa_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-toa-78">[76]</a></sup> The proposition is discussed among <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologians</a>, and proponents of <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">creationism</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">Galaxy cluster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illustris_project" title="Illustris project">Illustris project</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">Nebula</a></div>\n<p>The Universe is composed almost completely of dark energy, dark matter, and <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">ordinary matter</a>. Other contents are <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> (estimated to constitute from 0.005% to close to 0.01% of the total mass of the Universe) and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The proportions of all types of matter and energy have changed over the history of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> The total amount of electromagnetic radiation generated within the universe has decreased by 1/2 in the past 2 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> Today, ordinary matter, which includes atoms, stars, galaxies, and <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>, accounts for only 4.9% of the contents of the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck2013parameters_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-planck2013parameters-8">[8]</a></sup> The present overall <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of this type of matter is very low, roughly 4.5 &#215; 10<sup>&#8722;31</sup> grams per cubic centimetre, corresponding to a density of the order of only one proton for every four cubic meters of volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap_universe_made_of_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmap_universe_made_of-6">[6]</a></sup> The nature of both dark energy and dark matter is unknown. Dark matter, a mysterious form of matter that has not yet been identified, accounts for 26.8% of the cosmic contents. Dark energy, which is the energy of empty space and is causing the expansion of the Universe to accelerate, accounts for the remaining 68.3% of the contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck2013parameters_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-planck2013parameters-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkMatter_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkMatter-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-86">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="1149" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg/220px-Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg/330px-Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg/440px-Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Formation_of_galactic_clusters_and_filaments.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The formation of clusters and large-scale <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">filaments</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> model with <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>. The frames show the evolution of structures in a 43 million parsecs (or 140 million light-years) box from redshift of 30 to the present epoch (upper left z=30 to lower right z=0).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nearsc.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nearsc.gif/220px-Nearsc.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nearsc.gif/330px-Nearsc.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nearsc.gif/440px-Nearsc.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nearsc.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the superclusters and <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a> nearest to Earth</div></div></div>\n<p>Matter, dark matter, and dark energy are distributed homogeneously throughout the Universe over length scales longer than 300 million light-years or so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> However, over shorter length-scales, matter tends to clump hierarchically; many <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> are condensed into <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, most stars into galaxies, most galaxies into <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">clusters, superclusters</a> and, finally, large-scale <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">galactic filaments</a>. The observable Universe contains approximately 300 sextillion (3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">23</span></sup>) stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> and more than 100 billion (10<sup>11</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Typical galaxies range from <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarfs</a> with as few as ten million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> (10<sup>7</sup>) stars up to giants with one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/10%5E12" title="10^12">trillion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M101_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-M101-91">[89]</a></sup> (10<sup>12</sup>) stars. Between the larger structures are <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a>, which are typically 10&#8211;150 Mpc (33 million&#8211;490 million ly) in diameter. The <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> is in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a> of galaxies, which in turn is in the <a href="/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster" title="Laniakea Supercluster">Laniakea Supercluster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-92">[90]</a></sup> This supercluster spans over 500 million light-years, while the Local Group spans over 10 million light-years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> The Universe also has vast regions of relative emptiness; the largest known void measures 1.8 billion ly (550 Mpc) across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universe_content_bar_chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="140" data-file-width="215" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Universe_content_bar_chart.svg/220px-Universe_content_bar_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Universe_content_bar_chart.svg/330px-Universe_content_bar_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Universe_content_bar_chart.svg/440px-Universe_content_bar_chart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universe_content_bar_chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of the contents of the Universe today to 380,000 years after the Big Bang as measured with 5 year WMAP data (from 2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> (Due to rounding errors, the sum of these numbers is not 100%). This reflects the 2008 limits of WMAP\'s ability to define dark matter and dark energy.</div></div></div>\n<p>The observable Universe is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> on scales significantly larger than superclusters, meaning that the statistical properties of the Universe are the same in all directions as observed from Earth. The Universe is bathed in highly isotropic <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">radiation</a> that corresponds to a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_spectrum" title="Blackbody spectrum">blackbody spectrum</a> of roughly 2.72548 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fixsen_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fixsen-7">[7]</a></sup> The hypothesis that the large-scale Universe is homogeneous and isotropic is known as the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">cosmological principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> A Universe that is both homogeneous and isotropic looks the same from all vantage points<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liddle_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liddle-97">[95]</a></sup> and has no center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livio_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-livio-98">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_energy">Dark energy</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a></div>\n<p>An explanation for why the expansion of the Universe is accelerating remains elusive. It is often attributed to "dark energy", an unknown form of energy that is hypothesized to permeate space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles(a)_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-peebles(a)-99">[97]</a></sup> On a <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">mass&#8211;energy equivalence</a> basis, the density of dark energy (~ 7 &#215; 10<sup>&#8722;30</sup> g/cm<sup>3</sup>) is much less than the density of ordinary matter or dark matter within galaxies. However, in the present dark-energy era, it dominates the mass&#8211;energy of the universe because it is uniform across space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two proposed forms for dark energy are the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, a <i>constant</i> energy density filling space homogeneously,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carroll_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-carroll-102">[100]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field_theory" title="Scalar field theory">scalar fields</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(physics)" title="Quintessence (physics)">quintessence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moduli_(physics)" title="Moduli (physics)">moduli</a>, <i>dynamic</i> quantities whose energy density can vary in time and space. Contributions from scalar fields that are constant in space are usually also included in the cosmological constant. The cosmological constant can be formulated to be equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a>. Scalar fields having only a slight amount of spatial inhomogeneity would be difficult to distinguish from a cosmological constant.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_matter">Dark matter</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a></div>\n<p>Dark matter is a hypothetical kind of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> that is invisible to the entire <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>, but which accounts for most of the matter in the Universe. The existence and properties of dark matter are inferred from its gravitational effects on visible matter, radiation, and the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Large-scale_structure" title="Observable universe">large-scale structure</a> of the Universe. Other than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrinos" title="Neutrinos">neutrinos</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">hot dark matter</a>, dark matter has not been detected directly, making it one of the greatest mysteries in modern <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>. Dark matter neither <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_spectrum" title="Blackbody spectrum">emits</a> nor absorbs light or any other <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> at any significant level. Dark matter is estimated to constitute 26.8% of the total mass&#8211;energy and 84.5%  of the total matter in the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkMatter_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-DarkMatter-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planckcam_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-planckcam-103">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordinary_Matter">Ordinary Matter</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">Matter</a></div>\n<p>The remaining 4.9% of the mass&#8211;energy of the Universe is ordinary matter, that is, <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and the objects they form. This matter includes <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, which produce nearly all of the light we see from galaxies, as well as interstellar gas in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intergalactic_medium" title="Intergalactic medium">intergalactic</a> media, <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, and all the objects from everyday life that we can bump into, touch or squeeze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2-104">[102]</a></sup> As a matter of fact, the great majority of ordinary matter in the universe is unseen, since visible stars and gas inside galaxies and clusters account for less than 10 per cent of the ordinary matter contribution to the mass-energy density of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ordinary matter commonly exists in four <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">states</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phases</a>): <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>. However, advances in experimental techniques have revealed other previously theoretical phases, such as <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein condensate">Bose&#8211;Einstein condensates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fermionic_condensate" title="Fermionic condensate">fermionic condensates</a>.\n</p><p>Ordinary matter is composed of two types of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>: <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hooft_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hooft-106">[104]</a></sup> For example, the proton is formed of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Up_quarks" title="Up quarks">up quarks</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quark</a>; the neutron is formed of two down quarks and one up quark; and the electron is a kind of lepton. An atom consists of an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, made up of protons and neutrons, and electrons that orbit the nucleus. Because most of the mass of an atom is concentrated in its nucleus, which is made up of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryons" title="Baryons">baryons</a>, astronomers often use the term <i>baryonic matter</i> to describe ordinary matter, although a small fraction of this "baryonic matter" is electrons.\n</p><p>Soon after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, primordial protons and neutrons formed from the <a href="/wiki/Quark%E2%80%93gluon_plasma" title="Quark&#8211;gluon plasma">quark&#8211;gluon plasma</a> of the early Universe as it cooled below two trillion degrees. A few minutes later, in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>, nuclei formed from the primordial protons and neutrons. This nucleosynthesis formed lighter elements, those with small atomic numbers up to <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, but the abundance of heavier elements dropped off sharply with increasing atomic number. Some <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> may have been formed at this time, but the next heavier element, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, was not formed in significant amounts. Big Bang nucleosynthesis shut down after about 20 minutes due to the rapid drop in temperature and density of the expanding Universe. Subsequent formation of <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">heavier elements</a> resulted from <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton1983_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton1983-107">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particles">Particles</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg"><img alt="A four-by-four table of particles. Columns are three generations of matter (fermions) and one of forces (bosons). In the first three columns, two rows contain quarks and two leptons. The top two rows\' columns contain up (u) and down (d) quarks, charm (c) and strange (s) quarks, top (t) and bottom (b) quarks, and photon (&#947;) and gluon (g), respectively. The bottom two rows\' columns contain electron neutrino (&#957; sub e) and electron (e), muon neutrino (&#957; sub &#956;) and muon (&#956;), and tau neutrino (&#957; sub &#964;) and tau (&#964;), and Z sup 0 and W sup &#177; weak force. Mass, charge, and spin are listed for each particle." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/330px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/495px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/660px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Standard model of elementary particles: the 12 fundamental fermions and 4 fundamental bosons. Brown loops indicate which bosons (red) couple to which fermions (purple and green). Columns are three generations of matter (fermions) and one of forces (bosons). In the first three columns, two rows contain quarks and two leptons. The top two rows\' columns contain up (u) and down (d) quarks, charm (c) and strange (s) quarks, top (t) and bottom (b) quarks, and photon (&#947;) and gluon (g), respectively. The bottom two rows\' columns contain electron neutrino (&#957;<sub>e</sub>) and electron (e), muon neutrino (&#957;<sub>&#956;</sub>) and muon (&#956;), tau neutrino (&#957;<sub>&#964;</sub>) and tau (&#964;), and the Z<sup>0</sup> and W<sup>&#177;</sup> carriers of the weak force. Mass, charge, and spin are listed for each particle.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">Particle physics</a></div>\n<p>Ordinary matter and the forces that act on matter can be described in terms of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> These particles are sometimes described as being fundamental, since they have an unknown substructure, and it is unknown whether or not they are composed of smaller and even more fundamental particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFIp1-3_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-PFIp1-3-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Close-110">[108]</a></sup> Of central importance is the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a>, a theory that is concerned with <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetic</a> interactions and the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong</a> nuclear interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oerter2006_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oerter2006-111">[109]</a></sup> The Standard Model is supported by the experimental confirmation of the existence of particles that compose matter: <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>, and their corresponding "<a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>" duals, as well as the force particles that mediate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_interactions" title="Fundamental interactions">interactions</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/W_and_Z_bosons" title="W and Z bosons">W and Z bosons</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFIp1-3_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-PFIp1-3-109">[107]</a></sup> The Standard Model predicted the existence of the recently discovered <a href="/wiki/Higgs_boson" title="Higgs boson">Higgs boson</a>, a particle that is a manifestation of a field within the Universe that can endow particles with mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnyisiFAQ_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnyisiFAQ-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strasslerFAQ2_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-strasslerFAQ2-113">[111]</a></sup> Because of its success in explaining a wide variety of experimental results, the Standard Model is sometimes regarded as a "theory of almost everything".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oerter2006_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oerter2006-111">[109]</a></sup> The Standard Model does not, however, accommodate gravity. A true force-particle "theory of everything" has not been attained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberg2011_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberg2011-114">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hadrons">Hadrons</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">Hadron</a></div>\n<p>A hadron is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_particle" title="Composite particle">composite particle</a> made of <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> <a href="/wiki/Bound_state" title="Bound state">held together</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_force" title="Strong force">strong force</a>. Hadrons are categorized into two families: <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>) made of three quarks, and <a href="/wiki/Meson" title="Meson">mesons</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pions</a>) made of one quark and one <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiquark</a>. Of the hadrons, protons are stable, and neutrons bound within atomic nuclei are stable. Other hadrons are unstable under ordinary conditions and are thus insignificant constituents of the modern Universe.\nFrom approximately 10<sup>&#8722;6</sup> seconds after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, during a period is known as the <a href="/wiki/Hadron_epoch" title="Hadron epoch">hadron epoch</a>, the temperature of the universe had fallen sufficiently to allow quarks to bind together into hadrons, and the mass of the Universe was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>. Initially the temperature was high enough to allow the formation of hadron/anti-hadron pairs, which kept matter and antimatter in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a>. However, as the temperature of the Universe continued to fall, hadron/anti-hadron pairs were no longer produced. Most of the hadrons and anti-hadrons were then eliminated in particle-antiparticle <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilation</a> reactions, leaving a small residual of hadrons by the time the Universe was about one second old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allday2002_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allday2002-115">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>244&#8211;66</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leptons">Leptons</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">Lepton</a></div>\n<p>A lepton is an <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-integer_spin" title="Half-integer spin">half-integer spin</a> particle that does not undergo strong interactions but is subject to the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a>; no two leptons of the same species can be in exactly the same state at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> Two main classes of leptons exist: <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">charged</a> leptons (also known as the <i>electron-like</i> leptons), and neutral leptons (better known as <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>). Electrons are stable and the most common charged lepton in the Universe, whereas <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tau_(particle)" title="Tau (particle)">taus</a> are unstable particle that quickly decay after being produced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_energy_physics" title="High energy physics">high energy</a> collisions, such as those involving <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> or carried out in <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup>\nCharged leptons can combine with other particles to form various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_particle" title="Composite particle">composite particles</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Positronium" title="Positronium">positronium</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> governs nearly all of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, as it is found in <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and is directly tied to all <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical properties</a>. Neutrinos rarely interact with anything, and are consequently rarely observed. Neutrinos stream throughout the Universe but rarely interact with normal matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lepton_epoch" title="Lepton epoch">lepton epoch</a> was the period in the evolution of the early Universe in which the <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a> dominated the mass of the Universe. It started roughly 1 second after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, after the majority of hadrons and anti-hadrons annihilated each other at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Hadron_epoch" title="Hadron epoch">hadron epoch</a>. During the lepton epoch the temperature of the Universe was still high enough to create lepton/anti-lepton pairs, so leptons and anti-leptons were in thermal equilibrium. Approximately 10 seconds after the Big Bang, the temperature of the Universe had fallen to the point where lepton/anti-lepton pairs were no longer created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> Most leptons and anti-leptons were then eliminated in <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilation</a> reactions, leaving a small residue of leptons. The mass of the Universe was then dominated by <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> as it entered the following <a href="/wiki/Photon_epoch" title="Photon epoch">photon epoch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Photons">Photons</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photon_epoch" title="Photon epoch">Photon epoch</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Photino" title="Photino">Photino</a></div>\n<p>A photon is the <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quantum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and all other forms of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Force_carrier" title="Force carrier">force carrier</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>, even when <a href="/wiki/Static_forces_and_virtual-particle_exchange" title="Static forces and virtual-particle exchange">static</a> via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_photons" title="Virtual photons">virtual photons</a>. The effects of this <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> are easily observable at the <a href="/wiki/Microscopic_scale" title="Microscopic scale">microscopic</a> and at the <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_scale" title="Macroscopic scale">macroscopic</a> level because the photon has zero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a>; this allows long distance <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">interactions</a>. Like all elementary particles, photons are currently best explained by <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> and exhibit <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave&#8211;particle duality">wave&#8211;particle duality</a>, exhibiting properties of <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> and of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/particle" title="wikt:particle">particles</a>.\n</p><p>The photon epoch started after most leptons and anti-leptons were <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilated</a> at the end of the lepton epoch, about 10 seconds after the Big Bang. Atomic nuclei were created in the process of nucleosynthesis which occurred during the first few minutes of the photon epoch. For the remainder of the photon epoch the Universe contained a hot dense <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> of nuclei, electrons and photons. About 380,000 years after the Big Bang, the temperature of the Universe fell to the point where nuclei could combine with electrons to create neutral atoms. As a result, photons no longer interacted frequently with matter and the Universe became transparent. The highly redshifted photons from this period form the cosmic microwave background. Tiny variations in temperature and density detectable in the CMB were the early "seeds" from which all subsequent <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">structure formation</a> took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allday2002_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allday2002-115">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>244&#8211;66</span></sup>\n</p>\n<div aria-labelledby="Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks
